The time period covered\nby this model is 1985 through 2000.\nThe following sections contain:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nA definition of the scope of the model\nA discussion of the methodology used\nAn overview of total demand\nA summary of the estimated market segmentation by launch\nvehicle\nt A summary of the estimated market segmentation by user type\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Details of the STS market forecast\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A summary of transponder trends\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A model overview by mission category\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Detailed mission models\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Bibliography\n\xe2\x80\xa2 List of acronyms.\nThe model follows closely the format of the 1984 edition.\n\nSCOPE\nAll known non-NASA, non-DOD reimbursable* payloads forecast\nto be flown by non-Soviet-bloc countries** are included in this model\nwith the exception of Spacelab payloads and Small Self-Contained Payloads.\nCertain DOD-sponsored or co-sponsored payloads are included if they\nare reimbursable launches.\n\n*Those payloads (i.e., JEA, SSIP, Cooperative) for which NASA provides\nfree transportation in exchange for other considerations are not\nincluded.\n**Payloads of the People\'s Republic of China are included in the model.\n\nMETHODOLOGY\n\nA brief overview of the approach used in preparing the model\nis presented in this section.\nKey Assumptions\nFour major assumptions underlie all the work reported in this\nmodel. Additional specific assumptions for certain market areas are\nidentified later in the report.\nr\n\nAssumption 1: There will be no revolutionary technical,\neconomic, or social developments which will drastically alter current\npians. While both "High" and "Low" planned projections are made (see\nfollowing), the model does not attempt to encompass the full range of\npossible futures. Thus, major wars, drastic swings in the world economy,\nand totally unforeseen technological breakthroughs are not included.\n(Evolutionary technological improvements in both space-based and\nterrestrial technologies are considered in the development of the High\nand Low models.)\nAssumption 2: Both Shuttle and Ariane are technologically\nsuccessful and price competitive. Neither launch system has been\ntrouble-free, and an extended period of down time for either could\nsignificantly impact overall launch schedules. As of the time of\npreparation of this document (Spring 1985), the STS launch price for\nFY 90 and beyond had not been established. Major disparities between\nSTS and Ariane prices could lead to some customer shifts between the\ntwo vehicles. It appears very unlikely that total demand for launch\nof commercial communications satellites will be affected significantly\nby moderate changes in price.\n\nAssumption 3: The impact of U.S. commercial expendable launch\nvehicles is not considered in this model. At the time this document\nwas prepared, there were no known commitments to use these vehicles.\nThe entry of such vehicles into the marketplace will likely have a\nnegligible effect on total launch demand, but may affect the market\nshares of STS and Ariane. Although both the Soviet Union and mainland\nChina have offered expendable launch vehicles commercially to Western\nand third-world countries, there have been no takers to date; it is\ntoo early to determine the effect of these offers.\nAssumption 4: In the time frame of this model (1985-2000),\nthe major impacts of Space Station are likely to be in the MPS area\nand should be reasonably bounded by the High and Low models. The planned\ninitial operational capability date for Space Station is now around\n1992. There is little interest in this activity in the communications\nsatellite industry, and interest likely will not pick up until there\nis an operational capability with a demonstrated economic advantage.\nIt appears that any economic advantage would come in the form of reduced\nrequirements for launch services. Uncertainty in the MPS field is so\ngreat that a very wide range of possible demand is forecast. This range\nis believed adequate to cover a variety of operational scenarios. There\nmay, however, be significant differences in the -ways in which payloads\nare flown and MPS is accomplished when the Space Station becomes\noperational.\nNature of the Forecasts\nThe Outside Users Payload Model is created from the bottom\nup in the sense that data on discrete payloads are used to construct\nan overall demand model. The projections are based largely on information\nabout specific transportation requirements of specific users of space\ntransportation (e.g., INTELSAT) rather than on analysis of derived space\ntransportation demand created by a given end-market demand (e.g.,\ninternational telecommunications).\n\nIn general, the model is based only on demand and is not assumed\nto be capacity limited. However, in the near term, user plans may be\nbased on projected available capacity and may to some extent be capacity\nlimited.\nBecause both user plans and NASA needs are defined in terms\nof discrete payloads rather than average rates, this model also is\nexpressed in terms of discrete payloads. In the near term, the data\non the payloads themselves should be reasonably accurate. Near-term\nlaunch.forecasts are the best available, but there has been a considerable\nhistory of slippage in all launch schedules.* Substantially more latitude\nshould be given to the longer term forecasts. These might best be\ninterpreted as indications of probable activity levels in appropriate\ntime frames. At a minimum, it should not be inferred that the level\nof precision expressed in some of the tables is an indication of the\naccuracy of the forecasts.\nWhile the models draw heavily on specific user plans for data,\nsome adjustments have been made. These adjustments reflect the authors\'\njudgments on such matters as the likelihood of achieving a stated\nschedule, the impact of total market demands on the viability of all\nthe announced candidates to provide a given service, etc. In addition,\nadjustments in long-range demand are included in the form of follow-on\nand "market forecast" payloads. The follow-ons consist of payloads\nwhich may be used to replace and/or expand an existing service. Market\nforecasts are hypothetical payloads inserted in recognition of the fact\nthat there will be additional long-term demand which has not yet been\nidentified.\n\n*Typically, the number of actual launches achieved in a given year\nrepresents about 70 percent of the launches scheduled at the beginning\nof that year.\n\n"High" and "Low" Model Concepts\nAs in previous editions of this document, both "High" and\n"Low" models are presented. These two models are not intended to\nrepresent absolute bounds on any program or mission category, but it\nis believed that there is a reasonable likelihood that total activity\nwill, on the average, fall within the indicated bounds. (Historically,\nthe actual number of near-term launches has been much closer to the\nLow model forecasts than to those of the High one.) The two models\nthus define a range of potential demand which might be considered in\nmaking planning decisions.\nInherent in the High model are more optimistic assumptions\nof sustained growth in established demand and the early success of planned\nnew activities; e.g., direct broadcasting and materials processing.\nIn general, data included in this model are based on the authors\'\nassessment of announced plans, with additional market forecast payloads\nincluded in later years. Few program delays are assumed, as is a lower\nlevel of market penetration by competing terrestrial technologies.\nA reduction of the level of activity is assumed in the Low\nmodel. This could result from a variety of causes. Among them are\nslower growth in total demand; budgetary problems in government programs\nor financing problems in the private sector; technological improvements\nwhich allow demand to be met with smaller payloads or payloads with\nlonger life; capture of a part of the total demand by a competing\nterrestrial technology; and unanticipated schedule slips.\nNo attempt is made to force the number of payloads in the\nLow model for any given year in a category to be lower than the number\nof payloads in the High model for the same year and same category. This\nresults in an occasional anomaly where there may be more payloads in\na given year in the Low model than in the High. One example of this\ncan be found in Table 3 in the "Foreign Reg. Comrn." line for the year 1993\nwhich shows 7 launches in the High model; 8 in the Low. Hypothesized\nslips in launch schedules in the Low model is the explanation for this\n\nparticular entry. The intent of the labels "High" and "Low" is to\nidentify tendencies in the aggregate over the period covered by the\nOUPM; this is amply reflected by the total launches displayed at the\nend of the "Foreign Reg. Comrn." row: High = 141, Low = 86.\nData Sources\nA variety of data sources was used in preparation of the model.\nThe opinions of spacecraft manufacturers and other aerospace firms,\nspace system operators, NASA Shuttle mission planners, NOAA, and NASA\nlaunch vehicle planners were obtained. These contacts were made both\nby phone and in visits to the various organizations.\nAdditionally, data were obtained from the available literature\n(e.g., Aviation Week and Space Technology, Satellite Week, and\nInterspace). Other models and projections of future traffic and related\nareas (e.g., on-orbit transponder requirements) were reviewed for\nadditional data and insights into future space activity. Projected\nlaunch schedules for the Shuttle, Atlas/Centaur, Delta, and Ariane were\nused as guidelines for the early years of the model. A list of data\nsources is provided in Appendix B.\nWith the wide variety of data inputs and the continually\nchanging plans of the various organizations involved, there are, as\nwould be expected, a number of conflicts. Where possible, these conflicts\nare resolved via direct contact with the user organization. In other\ncases, judgment is used to resolve the conflicts. Since the data inputs\nreflect plans and intentions of the organizations, some judgments are\nmade as to the validity of the schedules and the potential for schedule\nslips.\n\nEstimation of Equivalent Shuttle Flights\nBecause the primary objective of this document is to provide\ninformation to Shuttle planners, an attempt is made to characterize\nall payloads in terms of equivalent Shuttle flights. The payloads\nincluded in the OUPM vary widely in size, weight, shape, destination,\nand are scheduled to be launched on a number of different launch vehicles,\nso the conversion is not necessarily a straightforward one.\nFor Shuttle payloads, the equivalent portion of a Shuttle\nflight is based on the estimated load factor as defined in existing\nreimbursement policies. Where both the length and weight of the payload\nare known, both length and weight load factors are computed and the\nlarger is used. Computation of the weight load factor is based on\nadvertised Shuttle capability to the intended orbit (e.g., 65,000 Ibs.\nto 28.5\xc2\xb0/160 nm, 32,000 Ibs to 90\xc2\xb0/160 nm). For payloads not yet well\ndefined, estimated Shuttle payload weight is used as the basis for\ncomputing load factor on the assumption that these payloads likely will\nbe Shuttle-optimized such that length and weight load factors are\nessentially identical.\nThe construction of the Shuttle equivalent load factors for\npayloads designed exclusively for flight on other launch vehicles is\nbased on determining the weight of a Shuttle cargo which could place\nthe same spacecraft weight in geosynchronous transfer orbit as the launch\nvehicle in question. For each vehicle, the maximum capability of the\nlauncher is used as opposed to the weight of a specific payload. In\ntranslating payload capability of a specific expendable launch vehicle\nto an equivalent Shuttle load factor, an equivalent Shuttle cargo weight\nis determined by assuming a PKM with a Isp of 285 seconds, an expended\nmass fraction of 0.91 (typical of a solid motor), and a cradle factor\nof 0.2. An additional correction is made to account for differences\nin launch site. A minimum equivalent load factor of 0.05 is assumed\nsince this is the minimum STS charge factor. The resulting load factors\nbased on these assumptions are shown in Table 1.\n\n8\nTABLE 1. If the\nload factor is greater than 0.75, the Shuttle flight equivalent is set\nto 1.0.\nThe method of computing equivalent Shuttle flights has a\nsignificant impact on the apparent demand. For example, implicit in\nthis approach is the assumption that the Shuttle is capable of carrying\nalmost five PAM-D class payloads. While it might be argued that this\nis an overstatement of Shuttle capability, the approach is consistent\nwith current shared-flight reimbursement practices. To the extent that\nthis approach overstates capacity, the mission models understate demand\nin terms of equivalent Shuttle flights.\n\n10\n\nOVERVIEW OF TOTAL DEMAND\n\nThis section presents a brief overview of total\nSpecific details and rationale are contained in later sections.\n\ndemand.\n\nComparison of 1984 and 1985 Models\nTable 2 provides a summary comparison of the 1984 and 1985\nmodels. It shows significant decreases in the number of payloads in\nboth High and Low models.\nThe number of payloads in the High model has decreased by\nalmost 12 percent, but the number of equivalent STS flights in the same\nmodel has decreased by less than 1.5 percent. The payload count is\nlower primarily because of reductions in the numbers of communications\nsatellites being projected for the 16-year period of the OUPM. Reasons\nfor these major reductions are presented in the sections containing\ndiscussions of these three mission categories. The number of equivalent\nSTS flights is not similarly impacted because of the addition of about\n19 STS flights in the materials processing category. More than half\nof these are attributable to adding CY 2000 to the model and dropping\nCY 1984 (there were no MRS payloads in 1984; there are enough predicted\nfor 2000 to require nearly 10 STS flights). The rest have been added\nat various points in the detailed MRS model and are discussed later\nin this report.\nThe reduction in number of payloads in the Low model is also\nabout 12 percent; however, the reduction in equivalent STS flights is\n9.6 percent in this case. Again, major decreases in the number of\ncommunications satellites are responsible for virtually the entire loss\nof payload count in the Low model. In this model, however, ,the MRS\ncategory is not nearly so dominant as in the High model; thus, there\nis a much more significant loss of equivalent STS launches.\n\n11\nTABLE 2. A brief category-by-category summary follows; details\nare provided in a later section.\nInternational Communications\nSignificant decreases in both numbers of payloads and equivalent\nSTS flights are shown in both High and Low models. These decreases\nare due primarily to two factors; a decrease in experienced and forecast\ngrowth for international communications and a major increase in announced\nfiber-optics communications capacity for both the Atlantic and Pacific\nOcean regions. This capacity could take most, if not all, of any\nincreased demand for international traffic in these regions.\nU.S. Domestic Communications\nBoth High and Low models show drastic reductions of payloads\nwhen compared to the 1984 OUPM. These reductions are attributed to\na variety of reasons: (1) delays in beginning new projects are due\nto difficulty in finding financing and delays in regulatory procedures;\n(2) there is no apparent great demand for new satellite communications\ncapacity in the near future; (3) advances in fiber-optics technology\nand announced domestic fiber-optic communications capacity portend a\nlow-growth future for satellites if the promise of fiber optics is\nfulfilled; (4) the direct-broadcast satellite segment of the market\nis apparently having difficulty defining a market, obtaining financing,\nand coping with pioneering an entirely new area of television delivery.\nBoth models also show decreases in equivalent STS flights,\nbut the decreases are not so large, percentagewise, as the decreases\nin numbers of payloads. Although number of payloads declined about\n30 percent in both models, the equivalent load factors declined only\nabout 18 percent. This potentially indicates a modest trend to larger\nsatellites.\n\n13\n\nForeign/Regional Communications\nAs in International and U.S. Domestic Communications, there\nhave been substantial reductions of payloads and equivalent STS launches\nin both the High and Low models. These changes are due to a number\nof reasons, the most substantial of which are: (1) reduction of projected\nChinese satellites based on a clearer perception of their plans,\n(2) reduction of "market model" satellites, based on the likelihood\nof. fewer of these unidentified entities being needed, and (3) projected\ncapture of a portion of this communications market by alternative means\nof transmission.\nU.S. Geostationary Earth Observation\nThe only changes from last year\'s model is the addition of\none GOES satellite launch in each of the High and Low models in accordance\nwith NOAA\'s current plans. Because of satellite size, these additions\nmake no significant impact on the number of equivalent STS flights.\nForej\'gn Geostationary Earth Observation\nChanges in this category are minor and are the result of\nperceived small changes in the ESA and Japanese programs.\nU.S. Low Earth Observation\nThe Low model shows negligible change from last year. The\nHigh model has been revised downward rather drastically to reflect the\nbelief that commercial LEO projects, aside from the commercialized\nLANDSAT, are not likely to occur in the quantity included in last year\'s\nOUPM, if at all.\n\n14\n\nForeign Low Earth Observation\nThe small decreases in payload numbers seen in this year\'s\nHigh and Low models are mainly due to reducing the numbers of satellite\nlaunches contained in the "market forecast" areas. Since these were\nrelatively small payloads, their deletions had no discernible effect\non the numbers of equivalent STS flights projected for this category.\nNavigation Aids\nThe only changes in this category are the addition of a proposed\ngeopositioning system in the High model, and the dropping of 1984 data\nand adding of CY 2000 data.\nForeign Planetary\nThere are no changes of significance in this category.\nScientific/Technical Development\nThere are minimal changes in this category. The payload changes\nthat were made were occasioned by obtaining better definition of the\nESA, Indian, and Japanese S/TD programs for the near and, particularly,\nmedium term. These changes are not significant in changing the number\nof projected equivalent STS flights in either the High or Low model.\nMaterials Processing\nIn the Low model, several additional small payloads were added\nin the area of pharmaceutical research; these made no impact on the\nnumber of equivalent STS flights. In the High model, however, there\nhave been large increases in both numbers of payloads and numbers of\nequivalent STS flights when compared to the 1984 OUPM. There is one\nmajor reason for these differences: in 1984, there were no materials\n\n15\n\nprocessing payloads included in the model; CY 2000, added in the 1985\nOUPM, contains two mature space production programs, each of which\naccounts for a number of very large payloads, as well as a variety of\nother programs with MPS payloads of varying size. Because MRS is still\na very speculative program in terms of both viability and schedule,\nvery large differences are shown between the High and Low models.\nMultiservice Spacecraft\nThe model remains largely unchanged, save for addition of\nCY 2000 data and deletion of 1984 data.\nAnnual Demand\nCumulative total demand, in equivalent Shuttle flights, is\nportrayed in Figure 1. Table 3 presents a breakdown of forecast total\npayload demand by year for each mission category. This same demand\nis displayed in equivalent Shuttle flights in Table 4.\nNear-term demand is almost the same in both High and Low models\nbecause virtually all launches in the first few years are essentially\nfirm. (It is worth noting, however, that past major slips in both Shuttle\nand Ariane programs have resulted in the launch rate of previous Low\nmodels not being achieved. As in past models, this model assumes that\nno further such slips occur.) Divergence of the models with time is\ntraceable to differences in rates of growth in virtually every payload\ncategory. Major contributors to the divergence are communications\nsatellites and payloads dedicated to materials processing activity.\nIt is also noted in Tables 3 and 4 that communications satellite\ndemand is somewhat cyclical, with a relatively high number of launches\nin a given period followed by a similar bulge about 10 years later as\nreplacement satellites are launched. Some reasons for these cycles\nare discussed in a later section.\nTable 5 presents total payload demand by type of user for\nreaders desiring this information.\n\n16\n\n240\n\no>\n\nI\n\n1\n\nI\n\n1\n\nI\n\nT\n\nT\n\nI\n\nI\n\nT\n\n200 -\n\nIU\n\n160\nc\n<u\n\n=\n3\n\nHIGH\n\n120 -\n\nCT\nOJ\n\nto\n<u\no\n<u\n\n80\nLOW\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nM\n03\n\n40 -\n\n1985\n\n1990\n\n1995\n\n2000\n\nYear\n\nFIGURE 1. Ul\na\n\nMO\n\na\nIU\n\n(A\n\nz\no\nM\n\noo\n\nooo\n\xc2\xbb\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nZ-l\n\noo\n\nu>\n\n01\n\nUl\n\no.\no\n\n.f^*.\n\nTHIS PAGE LEFT BLANK DELIBERATELY\n\n20\n\nMARKET SEGMENTATION BY LAUNCH VEHICLE\n\nA number of figures and tables in this section present an\noverview of potential market segmentation. Special emphasis is placed\non the Shuttle/Ariane market split. The approach used in assigning\nmarket segments is discussed.\nFigures 2 and 3 show a breakdown of the total market assigned\nto each category of launch vehicle. The shaded area on each figure\nrepresents those payloads for which Ariane/Shuttle competition is assumed\nto exist.\nTable 6 contains summary data which were used in the preparation\nof Figures 2 and 3. (Detailed data are contained in Appendix A.) Ariane\nand/or STS payloads were divided into five categories, and a probable\nmarket share was assigned to each category as indicated in the "STS %"\ncolumn. For example, it was assumed that 100 percent of the payloads\nin the "STS" class would fly on Shuttle, while only 25 percent of those\nin the "Ariane/STS" class would do so. These assignments formed the\nbasis for what might be termed "expected value" forecasts. That is,\nif a single payload were classed as "STS/Ariane", 3/4 of that payload\nwould be assigned to STS and 1/4 .to Ariane. While this obviously does\nnot represent physical reality, it does provide a reasonable basis for\nforecasting aggregate market splits.\nAssignments of STS and Ariane payloads were made according\nto the following general guidelines.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Payloads already under contract to fly only on a given vehicle\nwere assigned to that vehicle.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Payloads requiring the unique services of Shuttle were\nassigned to Shuttle, as were those of U.S. government agencies\nand those flown as part of a joint program with NASA.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Payloads of ESA and ESA member state governments were assigned\nto Ariane unless unique Shuttle, capabilities were required\nfor the launch.\n\n-PRECEDING PAGE BLANK NOT FILMED\n\nORIGINAL\nOF. The market segmentation\nactivity is better viewed in the aggregate than at the individual payload\nlevel. In some cases, the assignment of payload "X" to market segment\n"Y" was based on achieving a reasonable market balance rather than on\nspecific knowledge of that payload.\nTables 7 and 8 and Figure 4 provide a further breakdown of\nShuttle/Ariane competition. There is no guarantee\nthat Ariane will capture all payloads assigned to it, nor is there any\ncertainty that the predicted STS nominal share will be that actually\nachieved. Rather, these data should be interpreted as general indicators\nof likely market trends. Figure 4 shows the market sources, by mission\ncategory, of all payloads likely to be flown on either the Shuttle or\nAriane.\nCertain information contained in Tables 7 and 8 deserves\nhighlighting\xe2\x80\x94virtually the entire competition between STS and Ariane\nfor payload launches will concentrate on communications satellites.\nTable 7 shows that 168 of 188 potentially competitive payloads contained\nin the High model are accounted for in the three categories of\ncommunications satellites; in the Low model, 87 of 100.\nThis is\n89 percent and 87 percent of the market, respectively. On an equivalent\nShuttle flight basis (see Table 8), the percentages are 93 percent for\nthe High model and 91 percent for the Low model. So even though the\nprojected number of communications satellites was reduced substantially\nfrom last year\'s model, they still compose the overwhelming majority\nof the future payload launches expected to be the subject of competition\nbetween STS and Ariane.\n\nTHIS PAGE LEFT BLANK DELIBERATELY\n\n29\n\nSHUTTLE MARKET DETAILS\n\nBecause this document is prepared primarily for Shuttle planning\npurposes, some additional details on Shuttle demand are included here.\nTables 9 and 10 show estimated Shuttle demand. Optimistic,\nnominal, and pessimistic forecasts are included in both the High and\nLow models. The optimistic forecast is based on STS capturing all the\nflights assigned to it, plus all those identified as being STS-Ariane\ncompetitive. Expected-value results are indicated in the nominal case.\nPessimistic values assume that Shuttle will capture none of the\ncompetitive market.* The optimistic and pessimistic cases thus represent\nextreme conditions not likely to occur. They at least provide a rough\nindication of the market swings which could occur in a competitive\nenvironment.\nTables 11 and 12 indicate the nominal STS demand by year and\nmission category; Table 11 by payload, and Table 12 by equivalent Shuttle\nflights. Nominal demand is based on the expected value approach. As\nbefore, these tables should be viewed more as general indicators of\ndemand rather than as precise forecasts, especially in the later years\nof the program.\n\n*The ability of Ariane to handle all the inferred demand is not considered\nhere.\n\n30\nCM\n*\xe2\x80\x94 \'\n\n0\n\nr^l\n\n"re\no\n\nVO\n\nin o\nin cn\nr*^\n^^\nco CM\n\nO\n\nCM O\n\nr^\n\ncn TRANSPONDER TRENDS\n\nEstimates are presented here of the theoretical number of\noperating on-orbit transponders for each year of the time period covered\nby this model. These estimates provide a basis for comparison with\nforecasts of communications demand done by others. A convenient measure\nof communications capabilities in space is the equivalent 36-MHz\ntransponder.\nWhile it is sometimes difficult to equate a given\ncommunications requirement to 36-MHz transponders, estimates have been\nmade for all the communications satellites in the model. Data on\ntransponders have been grouped by bands: C, Ku, and Ka.* It is in\nthese bands that almost all of the standard communications traffic\n(civilian telephony and data transmission, TV and radio distribution)\nwill occur during the period covered by this model. In determining\nthe number of transponders on orbit over the time period of the model,\nthe first step was to tabulate data on satellites already in operation.\nThese satellites were assigned expected remaining lifetimes. Then,\nfor satellites in the model, the following assumptions were, made: (1) the\nsatellites are launched on schedule (if a satellite launch has failed,\nthe transponders on that satellite are not counted), (2) they operate\nas expected (i.e., no failures) and (3) they last for their predicted\ndesign lifetimes, no longer/no shorter. From these data, the number\nof active transponders on orbit is calculated.\nData on five different portions of the model are presented.\nThese are U.S. domestic, WARC Region 2 (primarily the Americas),\ninternational communications (i.e., INTELSAT), foreign/regional, and\ntotal world demand. These data are shown for both the High and Low\nmodels in Figures 5 through 9. In each figure, a breakdown by transponder\n\n*C-band is 6/4 GHz, Ku is 14/11 GHz, and Ka is 30/20 GHz. This model\ndoes not present data on transponders in other frequency bands for\nother communications purposes such as 8/7 GHz for military communications\nand 1.6/1.5 GHz for maritime.\n\nORIGINAL PAGE 15\nPOOR QUALITY,\n\n35\n6000\nHIGH MODEL\n\n\xc2\xbb\xc2\xab\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2*\n\nKu\n\xc2\xbb\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\nKo\nTotal\n\n85\n\n86\n\n87\n\n88\n\n89\n\n90\n\n91\n\n92\n\n93\n\n94\n\n95\n\n96\n\n97\n\n98\n\n99\n\n100\n\n4000\n\n<n\n\nLOW MODEL\n\n3000O\n\na.\n2000\xc2\xa3.....\nKu\n\n*****\n\n1000-\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nE\n\nKo\n\n3\n\nTotal\n\n85\n\n86\n\n87\n\n88\n\n89\n\n90\n\n91\n\n92\n\n93\n\n94\n\n95\n\n96\n\n97\n\n98\n\n99\n\n100\n\nFIGURE 5. THEORETICAL REGION 2 TRANSPONDERS ON ORBIT 1,2\n1\n\nIncludes only payloads contained in this model.\n\n"Assumes all launches succeed, all transponders operate for full design life.\n\n39\n4000\n\nHIGH MODEL\n\n\xc2\xa3.....\nKu m *\nmm\nKa\nTotal\n\n85\n\n86\n\n87\n\n88\n\n89\n\n90\n\n91\n\n92\n\n93\n\n94\n\n95\n\n96\n\n97\n\n98\n\n99\n\n00\n\n2000\n\nc\n\xe2\x80\xa2a\no\na.\n\n1500-\n\nLOW MODEL\n\n1000-\n\nI\n\n500-\n\n\xe2\x80\x94\xc2\xab\xc2\xabnr.lr.r.r.r.s.-..-.nr.sr.r.r.r.;\n\nKg\n\nTotal\n85\n\n86\n\n87\n\n88\n\n89\n\n90\n\n91\n\n92\n\n93\n\n94\n\n95\n\n96\n\n97\n\n98\n\n99\n\n00\n\n1,2\n\nFIGURE 9. THEORETICAL FOREIGN/REGIONAL TRANSPONDERS ON ORBIT\n\nIncludes only payloads contained in this model.\n2\nAssumes all launches succeed, all transponders operate for full design life.\n\n40\n\ntype is presented. These data provide insight into what communications\ngrowth is represented by the two models. They have not been used for\nfeedback to the model to approximate any preconceived growth curves.\nThe rationale for this is that estimation of transponder demand curves\nbased upon raw communications needs is beyond the scope of this effort\nand accepting any one projection over others is not justified. There\nis a natural tendency to compare these data with other forecasts, but\nthat is appropriately left to the reader.\nFigure 5, Theoretical Total World Transponders on Orbit,\nindicates that this year\'s projection is consistently lower than the\none contained in the 1984 OUPM. For 1985 and 1986, the projections\nare quite close to last year\'s, reflecting the fact that plans already\nunderway will likely not be revised in the near term. The growth rate\nin 1987-89 is considerably smaller than that shown in last year\'s model;\nthis is primarily due to low growth rates experienced by INTELSAT and\nU.S. domestic carriers and reflected in their launch plans for this\nperiod. By 1989, the 1985 High model projections are about 30 percent\nlower than shown in the 1984 model. From 1990 to 1997, the transponder\ngrowth parallels that shown last year, but is moved about two years\nto the right. In 1997 the shortfall has narrowed to about 15 percent.\nDuring the last three years of the period, the 1985 OUPM High model\nprojects little more than replacement, resulting in an increasing gap\nin projected number of transponders between the 1984 and 1985 models.\nThe Low model shows a steadily increasing divergence between the 1985\nand 1984 projections, with the 1985 model showing approximately one-third\nfewer transponders in 1997 than does the 1984 model. After about 1995,\nthe Low 1985 model shows replacement only. The High model shows a growth\nrate of about 10 percent during the overall period covered by the model,\nwhile the Low model shows about 5 percent growth in numbers of\ntransponders. Factors contributing to the lower numbers contained in\nthis year\'s OUPM include (1) in the near term, a slackening of current\nand projected growth rate of international communications services,\n\n41\n\n(2) about a two-year delay In an anticipated large increase in U.S.\ndomestic space communications capacity, and (3) the rapid emergence\nof fiber optics as a formidable competitor on all high-traffic routes\nin the medium and long term.\nInternational transponders,\nshown in Figure 6, exhibit\nsignificantly different behavior from that presented in the 1984 model.\nThe 1985 Low model shows international transponders reaching a total\nof nearly 1000 by 1987, then maintaining supply at about this quantity\nfor the rest of the century. This contrasts with last year\'s Low model,\nwhich reached about 1750 transponders in 1997 before leveling off. This\nLow model projection is a result of massive amounts of fiber-optic\ncommunications capacity (discussed elsewhere) that have been announced\nfor introduction to service in the 1988-90 time frame. In the High\nmodel, it is assumed that the fiber-optic networks come on line more\ngradually and that the space-segment providers of communications are\nable to compete more vigorously either by exploiting niche-market\nopportunities or by more competitive pricing strategies. The High-model\nfigures presented in Figure 6 show a growth rate of about 5 percent\nin the 1986-2000 period; this growth still\nresults in a\nturn-of-the-century shortfall of about a thousand transponders compared\nto projections of the 1984 OUPM. Another factor that may have to be\nconsidered in future models (but was not, in this model) is the\npossibility of the Soviet Intersputnik satellite system diverting a\nsignificant portion of third-world traffic from entities considered\nin the OUPM.\nFigure 7, Theoretical U.S. Domestic Transponders on Orbit,\nshows a High model picture that is not changed much from the 1984 High\nmodel. The major change is about a two-year delay, in the mid-term,\nin adding a large increase in capacity. Whereas the 1984 OUPM showed\nthis growth taking place between 1988 and 1991, the 1985 model shows\nit happening in the 1989-93 period. From 1993 to the end of the century,\nthe 1984 and 1985 High models show virtually identical growth, resulting\n\n42\n\nin about 3600 domestic transponders being the projection for the year\n2000. The two-year lag in capacity in the late 1980s and early 1990s\ncan be attributed to delays by the FCC in considering the large number\nof applications to provide both new and continuing services that were\nfiled before November 1983. As of the date of this publication, there\nhas been no FCC action on these applications, but such action is expected\nin Summer, 1985. A contributing factor to the lag is an apparent lack\nof demand for additional satellite capacity in the near term. The Low\nmodel transponder curve in Figure 7 shows a much slower growth picture\nfor U.S. domestic transponders. It shows an average growth of only\nabout 30 transponders per year for the 10-year period, 1987-96. It\nalso projects a total number of domestic transponders by the turn of\nthe century that is only about 40 percent of the number projected by\nthe High model. The Low model assumes that none of the new applicants\nfollow through on their plans to launch satellites and that fiber optics\ntakes the lion\'s share of growth in point-to-point communications within\nthe U.S. market. Any growth in provision of U.S. domestic communications\nsatellite services is assumed to be in point-to-multipoint traffic.\nThe depiction of Region 2 transponders, given in Figure 8,\nis about the same as in Figure 7 since U.S. domestic communications\nmake up 80-90 percent of the Region 2 total. This is true despite the\naddition of domestic systems for Mexico and Brazil, and the addition\nof a potential network for Agentina in the High model. Telesat Canada\nhas reduced its projected launches for the remainder of the century,\nso U.S. domestic transponders still dominate the numbers for Region 2.\nFigure 9 shows foreign and regional transponder projections\nfor the first time in an OUPM. The High model shows a relatively high\ngrowth (about 9 percent annually) between 1985 and 1998, with a leveling\noff after 1998 at about 1700 transponders. The Low model shows a more\nmodest growth rate of about 5 percent with a transponder count of about\n1000 by the year 2000.\n\n43\n\nMODEL OVERVIEW BY MISSION CATEGORY\n\nThe payload model is\nIn this section, a discussion\nlevels of activity, schedules,\nas well as changes from the 1984\n\ndivided into twelve mission categories.\nof each category presents rationale for\ndifferences between High and Low models,\nmodel.\n\nInternational Communications\nMajor changes from the 1984 OUPM are apparent in this category.\nThe 1984 High model included a total of 74 international communications\nsatellites; the 1985 High model, 59. The 1984 Low model showed 50\nsatellites; the 1985 Low model, 36. Most of the changes in these totals\nare due to reductions in projections for INTELSAT in the period covered\nby this OUPM. Several factors contribute to reduced projections for\nINTELSAT:\n(1) proliferation of proposed international\nfiber-optic\ncommunications networks, (2) lower current and projected growth of\ncommunications traffic in portions of the INTELSAT network, and\n(3)- increased likelihood of competition from other projected satellite\nnetworks.\nLast year\'s OUPM mentioned the TAT-8 fiber-optic link for\nthe North Atlantic. Since then a TAT-9 cable has been proposed and\ntwo additional entities have filed for permission to build fiber networks\non the same route; these are Tel-Optik, Ltd. and Submarine Light Cable\nCo. (SLC), both with desired initial operating dates of 1989. The FCC\nhas recently approved both of these companies\' applications for cable\nlanding rights. An industry analyst has said that, if all these plans\nare effected, there will be six times more capacity available for the\nNorth Atlantic route than current projections indicate will be needed\nby 1995. In the Pacific basin, Japan is planning to lay a fiber-optic\ncable between Hawaii and Japan to be operating by 1988, and AT&T and\n21 other communications companies are planning a fiber-optic cable between\nthe U.S., Hawaii, and the western Pacific to be operational in 1989.\n\n44\n\nOther fiber-optic-cable systems for the Pacific region are being\ndiscussed.\nINTELSAT can also expect competition from other organizations\nthat have applied for authorization to supply satellite-based\ncommunications capacity between Europe and North America, North America\nand South America, and North America and Asia. It appears that the\nFCC is close to granting approval to one or all of the applications\nby Orion Satellite Corporation,\nCygnus Satellite\nCorporation,\nInternational Satellite, Inc., and RCA Americom for service across the\nNorth Atlantic. Other intercontinental systems are not so far along\nin the process, but approval of the domestic North Atlantic competitors\nwill probably provide impetus for entities desiring to supply satellite\ncommunications in other regions.\nThe third development impacting this year\'s projections is\nthe fact that INTELSAT traffic growth is slowing down. From 1965 to\n1980, international satellite communications grew at greater than a\n15 percent annual rate, compounded. In 1984, use of INTELSAT circuits\nin the Atlantic Ocean Region (where a large majority of its capacity\nis located) grew by only 7 percent. Growth in other regions also did\nnot match earlier projections.\nThe result of the preceding factors has resulted in a slower\ngrowth scenario for INTELSAT in both High and Low models than was\npresented last year. The remaining satellites of the INTELSAT V (and\nV-A) series are already committed and will be launched by 1987 in both\nmodels.\nThe 1984 Low model projected only five large INTELSAT Vis.\nThis year, both models show only five I-VIs, the ones that have already\nbeen contracted for. These would be launched between 1986-90 in the\nHigh model, and 1987-91 in the Low.\nAn INTELSAT VII series is projected for both models, with\nfewer transponders than the I-VIs and weighing about 20 percent more\nthan the I-Vs. In the Low model, ten of these I-VIIs would be launched\nbetween 1991-2000. These launches would reflect a gradual loss of\ncapacity for INTELSAT during the latter half of this decade. In the\nHigh model, the I-VII series would consist of twelve satellites with\n\n45\n\nthese being launched 1990-95; an I-VIII series of eight satellites with\n20 percent more capacity than the I-VIIs would be launched 1996-2000.\nIn the high scenario, the I-VIIs and I-VIIIs would present about a\n6 percent annual market growth for INTELSAT.\nIn the High model, it is projected that one of the international\ncompetitors can find a niche market in the Atlantic region and would\nlaunch a three-satellite system in 1989-90 with slight growth and\nreplacement by the end of the century. The Low model shows the same\nsystem, but with no growth.\nA satellite communications system for the Pacific basin is\nshown, and is largely unchanged from last year\'s projection. It consists\nof a two-satellite system in the High model, starting with 1989 and\n1991 launches with replacement and growth by the end of the century.\nIn the Low model, the first satellite is launched\' in 1990; the second,\nin 1993; no growth is projected, only replacement. A two-satellite,\ninter-Americas system (PANAMSAT) is projected for 1991 in the High model;\nin the Low model, this system does not get past the planning stage.\nOne more international system exists: INMARSAT. There has\nbeen no change made from\' last year\'s model with the exception of\nreplacement satellites needed in CY 2000. Earlier this year, a team\nled by British Aerospace Dynamics Group (BA.DG) was awarded- a contract\nby INMARSAT for three satellites with options for six more. The High\nmodel assumes that these options are exercised and that the satellites\nare launched 1988-91; replacements are shown starting in 1998. In the\nLow model, a four-satellite system is projected with launches in 1989-92\nand replacements starting in 1999.\nU.S. Domestic Communications\nTotal numbers of U.S. Domestic communications satellites in\nboth the High and Low models of the 1985 OUPM show a dramatic decrease.\nThe High model goes from 163 to 111; the Low, from 97 to 69.\n\n46\n\nThese changes are a reflection of several developments of\nthe past year. Since the FCC has not yet acted on the majority of the\napplications received in November 1983, a number of potential launches\nhave been postponed from the 1987-88 period to later years. In some\ncases, this has meant that follow-on launches have slipped out of the\nOUPM horizon. Because of STC abandoning the DBS area and the difficulty\nof other groups in lining up firm financing, the number of projected\nDBS satellites has been dropped from 16 to 8 in the High model and from\n8 to none in the Low. The number of "market projection" satellites\nhas been reduced from 15 to 8 in the High model and from 7 to 4 in the\nLow, reflecting the belief that a good bit of new applications needs\nwill be met by capacity that is included elsewhere in the model. Finally,\nrapid strides are being made in fiber-optics technology along with a\nspeed-up in establishment of fiber-optics links, resulting in a general\nlowering of growth rate in domestic satellite communications after the\nearly 1990s.\nSeveral established U.S. commercial satellite systems for\ndomestic communications are currently in existence: they are WESTAR,\nSATCOM, TELSTAR, SBS, and Hughes Galaxy. In 1984, the first two GTE\nSpacenet satellites were launched, as well as two Syncom satellites\nthat are leased by the U.S. Navy. In 1985 the GSTAR and Amersat systems\nwill be initiated with launch of their first satellites. Follow-on\nand replacement satellites for these systems are projected for the period\ncovered by this OUPM.\nIn addition to these systems that are already established\nor in the process of becoming operational, there are a number of potential\nsystems that have applied to the FCC for authorization to build, launch,\nand operate domestic satellite communications systems. These applications\nare for three different types of service: (1) fixed satellite service\n(FSS), (2) broadcast satellite service (BSS), and (3) mobile satellite\nservice (MSS).\n\n47\n\nThe fixed satellite service refers to satellites operating\nin C, Ku and, eventually, Ka bands and providing telephone transmission,\ndata transmission, and distribution of network radio and television\nprogramming to subsidiaries and individual stations. All U.S. domestic\ncommunications satellites currently in orbit operate in FSS. Over the\npast several years, a number of potential new suppliers of communications\nservices in the FSS category have filed applications with the FCC.\nTable 13 presents a list of these entities, shows their status with\nthe FCC, and lists the number of satellites included in this issue of\nthe OUPM for the period 1985-2000. The systems included in the High\nmodel are viewed as having a reasonable chance of coming into existence,\nbased upon a high-growth scenario with fiber-optic competition\nencountering unforeseen difficulties.\nIn the Low model, a more\nconservative view of growth in user demand and development of new markets\nwas taken, as well as adopting the view of major growth by fiber-optic\ncompetitors, starting in the late 1980s. While a number of these\napplicants had expressed a desire to commence operations in 1987, the\nlack of positive action by the FCC to date makes it highly unlikely\nthat any of these applicants could have satellites in- orbit prior to\n1989.\nExamining Table 13 shows that the only actions the FCC has\ntaken on these applications have been negative ones; disapproval of\nthe Rainbow, ABCI, and USSSI applications. In addition, in May 1985,\nthe FCC proposed that any applicant to launch a new system or add\nsatellites to an existing system meet two criteria:\n(1) Unequivocally demonstrate that it can carry the financial\nburden of buying, launching, and operating the system\nfor one year.\n(2) Provide proof of customer commitments\nof the capacity of the proposed system.\n\nfor 80 percent\n\n48\nTABLE 13. Both High and\nLow models show replacement launches after 10 years.\nForeign/Regional Comrnunications\nThe category of Foreign/Regional Communications includes\nsatellites launched by single countries (private or government sponsored)\nand also satellites sponsored by organizations established to provide\nservices to a regional group of countries (e.g., Eutelsat). Certain\nsatellites with broader international services (e.g. INMARSAT) or\nsponsored by an organization whose .charter is to sell international\nservices (e.g., Cyprus) have been included in the International category.\nThe discussion of Foreign/Regional Comrnunications satellites is grouped\ninto three areas: Europe, Asia/Africa/Pacific, and Latin America plus\nCanada. The number of foreign and regional\nsatellites to be launched in the period covered by this OUPM have not\nchanged so dramatically as those for the international and U.S. domestic\nsectors. Nevertheless, the total number of payload launches has fallen\nfrom 170 to 141 in the High model and, from 98 to 86 in the Low. The\nmajor contributors to these reductions were clarification of China\'s\nfuture plans and provision of fewer satellites to currently unknown\nentities. Both Mexico and Brazil are shown with\nfollow-on launches in the mid-1990s in both High and Low models. The\nHigh model shows Argentina establishing a two-sate!lite domestic network\nin 1990-91, while the Low model assumes Argentina will continue to procure\nneeded satellite communications services from Intelsat or from one of\nits potential competitors.\nNone of the other proposed national or\nregional systems shown in Table 16 is included in either the High or\nLow models.\nPredicted and current demand for satellite communications\nin Canada has decreased drastically from earlier projections with the\nfollowing consequences:\n(1) The High model for 1985 projects 5 Telesat-Canada launches\nas opposed to 10 contained in the 1984 High model.\n(2) The equivalent figures for the Low model are 7 and 4.\n(3) Telesat\'s Anik C-3, launched in April of this year is\nplanned to remain inactive in on-orbit storage for about\n3 years before it is expected to be needed (it was launched\nbecause it was more economical to be stored on orbit than\nto be stored on the ground and launched during a period\nof considerably higher costs for launch services).\nAlthough there appears to be a good chance that Canada will proceed\nwith a mobile communications satellite (MSAT) to be coordinated with\nthe U.S. commercial Mobilsat program, there are indications that the\nlaunch of such a satellite would be underwritten partially or totally\nby NASA. This eventuality would class the launch of a possible Canadian\nMSAT as a NASA launch and, thus, not qualified for inclusion in this\nmodel.\n\n55\n\nIn 1985 several countries/organizations will join the list\nof Asian and African countries that have already orbited test and\noperational systems. Joining Indonesia, Japan, and China will be\nAustralia and the Arab Satellite Communications Organization. Table 16\nlists not only Arabsat and Aussat, but also a number of other systems\nbeing proposed, studied, or planned by Asian/African countries, groups\nof countries, and commercial organizations. Arabsat and Aussat each\nhas two launches scheduled in 1985; it is interesting that there is\napparently enough demand for the services to be provided by the Aussat\nnetwork that a third satellite in that series is already scheduled for\nlaunch in 1986. In the High model, both Aussat and Arabsat are shown\nwith replacement satellites including growth; the Low model indicates\nreplacement, only.\nAmong previously established entities in Asia, only Indonesia\'s\nprojected launches look much the same as last year. A Palapa launch\n(B-3) is scheduled for 1986; three replacements are scheduled for the\nearly 1990s in the High model, with only two in the Low model. This\nmodel predicts that an operational STW system for China will not be\nestablished so rapidly as predicted in the 1984 OUPM, with several\nlaunches being removed from both High and Low models. During the past\nyear, China\'s near-term plans for DBS have become clearer with its issuing\nan RFP for procurement of a two-satellite system, and reserving launch\nspace on both Ariane and STS. As a result, the numbers of DBS satellites\npredicted for Mainland China during the remainder of the century have\nbeen reduced by about half in both High and Low models. Japan is shown\nwith follow-ons to both their BS and CS series of satellites. In\naddition, the High model shows one of the recently formed Japanese-U.S.\ncommercial joint ventures launching a two-satellite network in the late\n1980s, and replacing it in the late 1990s. In the Low model, a totally\ndomestic Japanese system is shown in the early 1990s. While fiber-optic\ncommunication is not likely to affect the growth of communications\nsatellite systems in Indonesia and China in the near future due to the\nwidespread geographic dispersion of population in both of these countries,\n\n56\n\nit could have a severe impact in Japan due to (1) the compact nature\nof the Japanese nation and (2) the fact that Japan is a world leader\nin fiber-optic devices and systems.\nAmong smaller Asian countries, Pakistan and Korea are shown,\nin the High model, initiating domestic satellite communications networks\nin the 1990s. An African-Mediterranean area satellite (AMS) proposed\nby an Israeli consortium is also shown being launched in the early 1990s\nin the High model only. Although Thailand has been expressing possible\ninterest in buying and launching one of the recovered and refurbished\nHS-376 satellites, it could be considered to be included in the "market\nmodel" projections for other entities that could launch systems in the\nfuture.\nThe situation with respect to European area communications\nsatellites is largely unchanged from that presented in last year\'s OUPM.\nThe 1984 OUPM showed 57 satellites in the High model and 35 in the Low.\nThe 1985 version lists 59 in the High and 31 in the Low. Since the\ntotals are so close, only the relatively significant changes are\ndiscussed.\nESA has proposed a data relay system (DRS) consisting of three\nsatellites to be used in relaying information from the ESA Space Station\nmodule, Columbus, to Earth. This system would be launched in the\nmid-1990s in the High model, but is not contained in the Low. Italy\nis studying a three-satellite combined DBS-FSS system, called Sarit,\nto be orbited over a 7-year period starting in 1989. This system also\nis contained in the High model but not in the Low.\nA series of follow-on satellites to the British military\nSkynet IV system was proposed during the past year. This raised the\nnumber of launches .shown in the High model from four to eight, and in\nthe Low from four to six. Because Great Britain appears to be having\nboth commercial and political problems in establishing a DBS program,\nthe Unisat and Eiresat programs shown last year have been collapsed\ninto a single "UK DBS" system with High model launches reduced from\n6 to 4. Neither the 1984 or 1985 Low model envisions a DBS system\ndedicated to the United Kingdom.\n\n57\n\nThe nine-satellite ECS system of EUTELSAT shown in the 1984\nHigh model is reduced to six satellites in this year\'s issue, whereas\nthe Low model remains at six. An Olympus II satellite is now scheduled\nin both High and Low models; last year, only the first Olympus was shown\nin both models.\nThe remainder of the satellite systems shown in the 1985 OUPM\nin this category remain largely as shown in the 1984 version, with a\nfew launches being slipped a year or two, and an occasional satellite\nbeing added or dropped. There has been a name change: the German\n"Postsat" of last year is now "DFS-Kopernikus".\nAs a matter of policy, the French amateur radio satellite\n"Arsene" was dropped from the OUPM. These satellites are usually quite\nsmall compared to most payloads contained in this model. In addition,\nthey have little commercial significance, since they are usually launched\nat a minimal charge (or gratis). By including this type of satellite\nin an overall count of payloads launched, an artificially high sum is\narrived at. There will be no more of them included in the OUPM.\nOne idea, recently broached by EUTELSAT, but not included\nin this year\'s OUPM, is that of a Pan-European DBS system that would\nbe analagous to the ECS system in the FSS band. Since this proposed\nplan is so new it has had no reported reaction yet, it was not factored\ninto this year\'s OUPM.\nAs is seen from Table 16, there are two newly proposed systems\n(both Swedish) for which no satellites have been included in either\nHigh or Low model. One is the low-Earth-orbit Mail star message-relay\nsystem, and the other is a proposed business data-transfer system.\nU.S. Geostationary Earth Orbit (GEO) Observations\nGOES (Geostationary Operational Environment Satellite) is\na system operated by NOAA which provides data to weather stations\nthroughout the entire U.S. The system undergoes periodic improvements\nby installation of additional sensors and/or new improved sensors on\n\n58\n\nthe next series of satellites. The design life of the GOES spacecraft\nis 7 years. In the High model, replacements are launched every 5 to\n6 years to keep three active satellites on orbit with an in-orbit spare,\nif possible. In the Low model, fewer launches are forecast under the\nassumption that the replacement time is 7 to 8 years due to satellites\nexceeding their design life. There are no significant changes from\nthe 1984 OUPM.\nForeign Geostationary Earth Orbit (6EO) Observations\nTo the -present time only Europe and Japan have built and\nlaunched satellites for observing the Earth from geosynchronous orbit.\nBoth Europe and Japan launched their first such satellites for\nmeteorological purposes in 1977.\nThe Japanese GMS-1 (Geostatic\nMeteorological Satellite) launch was followed up with replacement launches\nin 1981 and 1984.\nFuture replacement launches are expected in 1989,\n1994, and 1999 in the High model. The Low model projects that a 7-year\nlifetime will be achieved for these follow-on satellites, resulting\nin a need for only two additional launches by the end of the century.\nAn experimental satellite to test instruments for a more advanced version\nof the GMS for the early 2000s is forecast for launch in the late 1990s\nin both High and Low models. Japan is also expected to launch a satellite\nto geosynchronous orbit to be used for geological and other resource\nobservations about 1990 in both models with a follow-on in about 7 years\nin the High model, and an experimental satellite toward the end of the\ncentury in the High Model. Japan is also projected to launch two\nadditional geostationary Earth observations satelites in both models\nin the 1990s; marine observation is a possible use for these satellites.\nThe European EUMETSAT program is planning to launch a prototype\nof a Meteosat Operational Program satellite in 1986, with launches of\na three-satellite network projected for 1987, 1988, and 1991.\nReplacements are forecast for seven years after the original satellites\nare launched. The Low model projects a two-satellite system launched\nin 1987 and 1988 with follow-ons eight years later. It is expected\n\n59\n\nthat ESA will begin a geostationary Earth observations program in the\nmid-1990s, and three satellites are projected in the High model, only\none in the Low.\nIt is expected that China will become the third foreign entity\nto launch geostationary Earth observations satellites. The High model\nprojects a single satellite in 1990, with replacements at 5-year\nintervals; the Low model delays initial launch to 1991, again with a\n5-year replacement schedule.\nFive additional foreign geostationary Earth observation\nsatellites are included in the High model, and three in the Low model,\nin anticipation of other national entities initiating their own domestic\nor regional systems.\nThe Low model scenario assumes that improvements in reliability\nand lifetime will reduce the follow-on launch rate. In addition, part\nof the reduction in activity is attributed to budgetary constraints\nwhich impact the initial R&D activities and force a stretchout of\nreplacements for the operational meteorological satellite systems.\nU.S. Low Earth Orbit (LEO) Observations\nPayloads in this category generally operate in the altitude\nrange of 210 to 600 nmi and are usually sun-synchronous. The service\nlife of these satellites is generally short compared to communications\nsatellites. The short service life results from economic trade-offs\nof the following parameters:\n(1) fuel required to maintain desired\norbit, (2) service lives of special instruments, (3) reliability of\nsystem components, (4) total power requirements, and (5) obsolescence\ndue to significant improvement in sensors, on-board data processing,\nand new techniques for reducing processed-data costs. A planned STS\noperation which may change the design requirements of LEO payloads is\nthe retrieval of LEO payloads for resupply. It is believed to be\npremature to factor the impact of these planned operations into either\nthe High or Low models.\n\n60\n\nSatellites in this category traditionally have been supported\nby NASA. They provide valuable data for a large portion of the\npopulation. The TIROS-N series of weather satellites has been transferred\nto NOAA, and NASA\'s LANDSAT system is in transition to a commercial\noperation. The High model shows the launch of the first commercial\nLANDSAT occurring in 1988 with a two-satellite system being established\nby 1991.\nFour replacement launches are also shown in the 1990s at\nintervals that could provide some backup capability in case of premature\nsatellite failure. The Low model shows a first commercial LANDSAT launch\nin 1989 with replacement launches occurring at five-year intervals.\nThe High model includes a postulated low-cost, short-life\nseries of LEO satellites customized for geological investigations for\nindividual corporate users. This satellite series is not included in\nthe Low model.\nForeign Low Earth Orbit (LEO) Observations\nTo date, the U.S. LANDSAT program has been the only operational\nLEO Earth observations program. This monopoly will be broken when the\nFrench SPOT-1 satellite is launched toward the end of 1985. A number\nof other countries and ESA are also planning to launch LEO satellites\nduring the remainder of the century for a variety of purposes.\nThe SPOT program is poised to take advantage of a two-year\ngap in LANDSAT operations that could occur as a result of the prolonged\nprocess of selecting a commercial operator to assume the U.S. Earth\nobservations program. A commercial marketing organization, SPOT-Image,\nwas formed in 1982 to market imagery and other products to be obtained\nand enhanced from the SPOT satellites. The High model predicts five\nlaunches of SPOTs, while the Low model projects four. ESA proposes\na series of ERS (Earth Resources Satellite) spacecraft whose primary\nmissions will be observations of ice cover, oceans, and weather. The\nHigh model shows four of these starting in 1989; the Low model, three,\nbeginning in 1990. Other European LEO satellites such as an Advanced\n\n61\n\nLand-1 for a 1995 launch are included in a generic "Operational LEO"\ncategory that contains two launches in the last half of the 1990s in\nboth High and Low models. The oft-discussed Franco-German military\nreconnaissance satellite, SAMRO, is projected to have seven launches\nin the High model, while it is absent from the Low model.\nJapan is planning a series of Maritime Observation Satellites\n(MOS) with seven launches shown in the High model and five in the Low,\nstarting in 1986.\nBoth models show a Japanese experimental geodetic\npayload being launched in 1986. In the 1990s, Japan has plans for a\nseries of Earth remote sensing (J-ERS) satellites that would be slanted\ntoward LANDSAT-type rather than ocean-sensing applications. The High\nmodel shows five of these satellites; the Low model, only three. In\nall of these Japanese LEO satellites, the first of the series is an\nexperimental, proof-of-concept satellite, with operational ones being\nbuilt and launched only after successful demonstration of the originals.\nA series of Indian remote sensing (IRS) satellites is shown\nwith a planned first launch on a Soviet ELV in 1986; five follow-on\nlaunches, to be carried out with the Indian-developed PSLV starting\nin 1988, are shown in the High model. The Low model projects only three\nfollow-on IRS launches by the end of the century. Two series of South\nAmerican and Chinese LEO Earth-observation satellites are also anticipated\nwith the first Chinese launch occurring in 1986. The first South American\nlaunch, possibly by Brazil (which has plans to develop its own launch\nvehicle), is not expected until 1990 in the High model and 1991 in the\nLow.\nThere are three other LEO satellites shown in the High model,\nbut not in the Low. These are a two-satellite POPSAT (Precise Orbit\nPrediction Satellite) being studied by ESA for 1992 and 1996 launches,\nand the on-again, off-again TERS (Tropical Earth Resources Satellite)\nbeing put forth as a joint venture of Indonesia and the Netherlands\nwith a projected 1996 launch in the High model. In addition, the High\nmodel contains six and the Low model three satellites to account for\ncountries that might decide to get into the LEO business, but have not\nyet made their interest known.\n\n62\n\nNavigation Aids\nThis category shows little change from the numbers that were\npresented last year. The only change is the addition of a commercial\nposition-determining system in the High model. The only other satellites\nare those of the NAVSTAR system being studied by ESA, and the U.S. Navy\'s\nTransit satellites.\nSince the Transit spacecraft have, historically, exceeded\ntheir design lifetime of 4 years by large margins, their actual launches\nhave been fewer than scheduled. The U.S. Navy has decided to launch\nthe remainder of the Transits and maintain them in on-orbit storage\nuntil needed. To this end, the SOOS (Stacked Oscar On Scout)* Program\nwas initiated to enable two Transits to be launched by a single Scout.\nThe High model shows eight Transits being launched in the 1985-87 period,\nin accordance with the Navy\'s planned schedule. The Low model contains\nthe same number of satellites, but assumes the SOOS program is not\neffected until 1988 and spreads the total program out until 1994. The\nNAVSAT system currently being studied by ESA is similar to the DOD\'s\nNAVSTAR project, but with simpler spacecraft. The High model assumes\nthat ESA adopts the program and deploys 27 spacecraft (including three\non-orbit spares) over a 5-year period beginning in 1990. The Low model\ndoes not include a NAVSAT system.\nThe High model also contains a position-locating satellite\nsystem being established in 1989-90, with replacement launches seven\nyears later. Four companies have applied to FCC to be considered for\nlaunching satellites in the RDSS (Radio Determination Satellite System)\nspectrum.\nThey are (1) Geostar Corporation; (2) MCCA American\nRadiodetermination Corp.; (3) McCaw Space Technologies, Inc.; and\n(4) Omninet Corporation. Geostar has carried out ground tests of its\nconcept and has applied to FCC for permission to place an experimental\n\n*0scar is the Navy\'s name for Transit.\n\n63\n\npayload aboard the GSTAR A-2 satellite to be orbited in 1986. The Low\nmodel includes no such satellites under the assumptions that (1) orbital\ntests prove unsuccessful, (2) there is no market for the service, or\n(3) the function can be provided with auxiliary payloads included aboard\nsatellites operating in the fixed-satellite service.\nForeign Planetary\nThis category is little changed from the 1984 model. Foreign\nplanetary missions currently are being planned by two organizations:\nESA and Japan. ESA has firm plans for two missions, Giotto (a Halley\'s\ncomet mission) and International Solar Polar, for the mid-1980s. With\ntheir general interest in this area, additional missions could occur\nin the 1990s, and one is included in both the High and Low models in\n1998. The ISPM mission is not included in the model since it is a\ncooperative mission with NASA providing the STS launch at no charge\nto ESA. The Japanese also have specific plans for the future and are\nlikely to continue their program into the 1990s. The Japanese are\nexpected to use one of their national launchers for these missions.\nThe High model includes three additional planetary missions before the\nturn of the century, while the Low model lists only one.\nScientific/Technical Development\nThere are few differences between the 1984 OUPM and this year\'s\nissue in the S/TD category outside of four payloads added in the High\nmodel and two in the Low because of the one-year change in the period\nbeing covered.\nThe majority of payloads in this category are sponsored by\nESA, Japan, and India. The High model projects that each of these\nsponsors will have expanding scientific programs in the 1980s and will\nmaintain a high level of activity using automated payloads in addition\n\n64\n\n.to Spacelab. Only the automated payloads are included in this model.\nESA has a relatively constant budget for science missions (adjusted\nfor inflation), thus the overall ESA activity remains relatively constant\nthroughout the time period.\nIn the Low model, it is assumed that Spacelab will be the\nfocal point of the ESA activity, with less emphasis on automated payloads.\nA trend toward cooperative missions may further reduce the number of\nESA scientific automated payloads.\nIn Japan, the Institute of Space and Aeronautical Sciences\n(ISAS) is responsible for carrying out the space-science program. As\nthe Japanese space-science program expands, it is projected that the\npayloads will increase in mass and size, and will utilize the capabilities\nof the N series launch vehicles rather than the smaller Mu vehicles.\nThe number of Japanese scientific satellites and the projected launch\nschedule were derived from the proposed Japanese 15-year space program\nplan. The proposed program, which is considered ambitious, was reduced\nslightly and used for the High model. The Low model reflects a greatly\nreduced level of activity for the Japanese scientific programs.\nIndia is planning an increasingly active program of scientific\nspace exploration. Since 1979, India has launched three of its Rohini\nscientific satellites using its own SLV-3 launch vehicle. The High\nmodel projects 15 additional Indian S/TD satellite launches during the\n16 years covered by the OUPM. The majority of these launches would\nuse the Indian-developed ASLV (ready this year) and PSLV (to be\noperational about 1988) expendable launch vehicles. The ASLVs would\nbe used to launch stretched versions of the Rohinis (SROSSs); the PSLV,\nwith more capability, is planned to be used for sun-synchronous launches\nof payloads weighing up to about 1300 pounds. The Low model projects\na total of ten Indian S/TD launches, six of them being SROSS-type\npayloads.\n\n65\n\nA Chinese S/TD payload program is also postulated, with six\nlaunches being contained in the High model and four in the Low.\nEmerging scientific and technical development launch programs\nare projected for both India and China. It is expected that payloads\ndeveloped under these programs will be launched on domestic Indian and\nChinese ELVs.\nMaterials Test and Processing\nThe materials processing mission category has the potential\nof becoming one of the major segments of the overall model. However,\nthere are still a great number of uncertainties regarding the commercial\nviability of materials processing in space (MPS). Consequently, there\nis a large difference between the High and Low models.\nThe availability of Space Station and the cost of doing business\nboth could be big factors in determining the economic feasibility of\nMPS and how it is performed. Given the uncertainties associated with\nSpace Station today, it was decided that no attempt would be made to\ndifferentiate between operations with and without Space Station.\nLEASECRAFT/EURECA-type operations . therefore are assumed through the\nmodel, except for a few attached R&D payloads. The High and Low models\nappear to contain sufficient latitude to deal with a wide variety of\nother possible outcomes.\nThe NASA Joint Endeavor Agreement (JEA) program will likely\nbe the beginning of many commercial operations. The initial JEA was\nestablished with McDonnell Douglas Astronautics Company and Johnson\nand Johnson (MDAC/JJ) for their Electrophoresis Operations in Space\n(EOS) program. The MDAC/JJ EOS program began in 1976 and had its first\nflight in the Shuttle mid-deck in 1982. Additional JEA flights have\nbeen made and others are scheduled in the 1984-1986 time period. These\nJEA flights, being non-reimbursable, are not shown in the model. The\nEOS models for 1985 are identical to those of 1984. Flights to date\n\n66\n\nare reported to have verified expected performance, and there are no\nannounced reasons to change the 1984 numbers. In the High model the\nEOS program is shown as beginning commercial operations in the late\n1980s and growing to full-scale operation in the 1990s. In the Low\nmodel, however, the EOS program is shown as being tried in the 1980s\nand not being continued. A number of factors could cause this. They\ninclude technical problems, economic viability, and development of a\nless expensive ground-based system. In both the High and Low models,\nit is assumed that a mix of shared and dedicated flights will be used\nin conjunction with the use of factories aboard free-flying space\nplatforms that can rendezvous with the Orbiter to exchange processed\nproduct for a resupply of raw materials. Fairchild (with Leasecraft),\nRCA, and Ball Aerospace are proposing such platforms.\nAlthough there are a large number of other products/processes\nthat have been considered for materials processing in space, only a\nfew have been identified as having a chance at becoming commercial\nventures. These are production of Gallium Arsenide chips and Mercury\nCadmium Telluride detectors, an isoelectric-focusing-separation technique\nwhich would complement EOS, and small-scale research programs in\nmetals/ceramics and protein crystals which would be used to better\nunderstand ground processes. In the High model, all these possibilities\nare included; while in the Low model only one is shown as being\ncommercially successful in competition with evolving terrestrial\ntechnologies. Since Pharmaceuticals appear to hold promise for producing\nhigh value-added products through space processing, a number of\npilot-plant size payloads are shown in the Low model. In the High model\nit has been assumed that sufficiently positive results are obtained\nin early JEA-supported flights to move immediately to production-size\npayloads in several cases; thus, no pilot plant payloads are shown on\na separate line in the High Model.\nOne additional generic MRS series involving relatively high\ntransportation demand is included in the High model. This demand could\nrepresent the later entry of a competitor in the EOS field or evolution\nof a major new product through JEA activities by firms such as 3M.\n\n67\n\nIn the foreign area, ESA\'s EURECA mission is included in both\nmodels, as well as some generic materials processing, which could be\nan outgrowth of German, French, or Japanese programs.\nMultiservice Spacecraft/Vehicle\nThe first operational satellite to operate in a multiservice\nmode was India\'s INSAT-1A, launched in 1982. This satellite, operating\nin geostationary orbit, provided communications services, TV broadcasting,\nand meterological observations before its premature demise after less\nthan five months of service. INSAT 1-B was launched in August 1983,\nand is functioning properly. The communications functions of this\nsatellite have been designed to be compatible with Indonesia\'s\nsecond-generation communications satellite PALAPA-B.\nThus, for the\ncommunications task, INSAT can back up PALAPA and vice versa. West\nGermany\'s deployable Shuttle Pallet Satellite (SPAS) is included in\nthis category since it has the capability of accepting a variety of\ndifferent types of payloads.\nIn future issues of the OUPM, more payloads may be included\nunder this category. As an example, several of the applicants for\nauthorization to build and launch satellites for mobile communications\nincluded requests to provide position determination capability aboard\nthe same satellites or filed separately for RDSS satellites. As this\nissue becomes clearer, reassignment of such satellites may be made.\n\nAPPENDIX A\nto\n\nOUTSIDE USERS PAYLOAD MODEL\nDETAILED PAYLOAD MODELS\nJULY 1985\n\nA-l\n\nAPPENDIX A\nDETAILED PAYLOAD MODELS\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nDetailed High and Low payload models are found In this appendix. Tables A-1A and A-IB present\nthe High model and Tables A-2A and A-2B present the Low model. In each case, the "A" tables contain schedule,\nlaunch, and organizational data; while the "B" tables contain technical details of the payloads themselves.\nDefinitions of the symbols used are given in the "Key to Tables and Table Symbols".\nIn some cases, a single type of payload may be represented by multiple entries in the tables.\nTypical examples would include:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A single series of payloads, some of which are committed to Shuttle and others committed to Ariane\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A series of payloads which transitions from one form of ownership to another during the forecast\nperiod.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A series of related payloads (e.g., MRS) which have a variety of launch weights.\nFairly detailed technical data are provided (in the "B" tables) on near-term payloads and others\nthat are well defined. Estimated values are frequently used for longer-term payloads.\n\nA-3\n\nDESIGN LIFE: Design service life of the satellite system is shown in years. These data are needed to estimate\nreplacement schedules.\nLAUNCH SCHEDULE: Number in column is the quantity per calendar year, not the month in which a launch is\nplanned.\nGROUND SPARE: Ground spares are not counted in any of the totals. The entry is included to provide\naccountability for the total buy of flightworthy satellites. Launches of on-orbit spares are not\ndifferentiated from the launches of active satellites.\nPROGRAM STATUS: Program status is provided to assist the user in assessing the level of commitment to the\nprogram and the certainty of the launch schedule. Frequently these data are missing due to data unavailability\nand/or lack of clarity in categorizing the status.\nA\nC\nD\nF\nL\nM\n0\nP\nS\n\n- Program approved\n- Contracted\n- Design/competition stage\n- Expected follow-on\n- Already launched within the year of the OUPM\n- Market forecast\n- Option\n- Proposed/planned\n- Being studied.\n\nA-5\n\nLAUNCHER STATUS:\n\nA\nC\nF\nP\nU\n\n- Alternative\n- Captive (no choice)\n- Firm\n- Probable\n- Unknown\n\nIf a single launch vehicle is shown and is assigned "F", "C", or "P", it is assumed that the vehicle will\nget 100 percent of the market. Where two launch vehicles are shown, the nominal market split is assumed\nto be 75/25 if one vehicle is listed as "P" and the other is listed as "A". Where both are "A", a 50/50\nnominal split is assumed. This does not mean that the individual payload\'s flights will be split as indicated.\nRather, it is a rough guess of which way the market for payloads of this type will be split. (In some cases,\na single type of payload may be flown on more than one launcher.)\nTRAJECTORY: Payloads destined for Geostationary Orbit will not have a specific transfer orbit stated.\nSun-synchronous orbits may not have altitude and inclination specified.\n\nKey to Tables A-IB and A-2B\nNOTE: The "B" tables contain a number of physical payload parameters: dimensions, weights, etc. These\nparameters are not always readily available and frequently cannot be estimated with any degree of certainty.\nA short dash -- or OPEN means data not available. A question mark indicates that data missing from this\nreport should be available from other sources.\n\nA-7\n\nSPACECRAFT DESCRIPTION: Describes type of bus or spacecraft to be used.\nEQUIVALENT 36-MHz TRANSPONDERS: For communications satellites, an estimated number of equiavalent 36-MHz\nC, Ku, and Ka band transponders is shown.\nSPACECRAFT WEIGHT: Weight, Ibs, of the spacecraft at the beginning of its operational life.\nCONFIGURATION FOR STS LAUNCH:\nparameters are known.\n\nAppropriate data (below) are indicated for those payloads where STS launch\n\nUPPER STAGE USED: Where perigee stages are used, indicates name or type to be used.
